  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5e964b53-4284-4955-afec-9226ef184728'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10658587995585006116">Zach is eighteen. He is bright and articulate. He's also an alcoholic and in rehab instead of high school, but he doesn't remember how he got there. He's not sure he wants to remember. Something bad must have happened. Something really, really bad. Remembering sucks and being alive - well, what's up with that? <br/><br/><i>I have it in my head that when we're born, God writes things do</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10658587995585006116" style="display:none">Zach is eighteen. He is bright and articulate. He's also an alcoholic and in rehab instead of high school, but he doesn't remember how he got there. He's not sure he wants to remember. Something bad must have happened. Something really, really bad. Remembering sucks and being alive - well, what's up with that? <br /><br /><i>I have it in my head that when we're born, God writes things down on our hearts. See, on some people's hearts he writes Happy and on some people's hearts he writes Sad and on some people's hearts he writes Crazy on some people's hearts he writes Genius and on some people's hearts he writes Angry and on some people's hearts he writes Winner and on some people's hearts he writes Loser. It's all like a game to him. Him. God. And it's all pretty much random. He takes out his pen and starts writing on our blank hearts. When it came to my turn, he wrote Sad. I don't like God very much. Apparently he doesn't like me very much either.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10658587995585006116" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_10861849').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_10861849').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_10861849').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {

              <span id="reviewTextContainer85130152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12634731256693666679"><b>Posted at <a href='http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2013/12/last-night-i-sang-to-monster.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shelf Inflicted</a></b><br/> <br/>Zach is a high school senior. He is also an alcoholic. His mom is depressed, his dad is an alcoholic, and his brother is abusive. Zach has so much pain in his young life that he blocks out the events that led to his stay in rehab. <br/><br/>With the help of a kind and sympathetic therapist, and his roommates, Rafael, a 53-year-old alcoholic further along in his recovery, and Sharkey, a 27-year-old drug addict, Zach begins to confront his past and discovers that life is worth livi</span>
  <span id="freeText12634731256693666679" style="display:none"><b>Posted at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2013/12/last-night-i-sang-to-monster.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelf Inflicted</a></b><br /> <br />Zach is a high school senior. He is also an alcoholic. His mom is depressed, his dad is an alcoholic, and his brother is abusive. Zach has so much pain in his young life that he blocks out the events that led to his stay in rehab. <br /><br />With the help of a kind and sympathetic therapist, and his roommates, Rafael, a 53-year-old alcoholic further along in his recovery, and Sharkey, a 27-year-old drug addict, Zach begins to confront his past and discovers that life is worth living.<br /><br />I connected deeply with Zach and his rehab companions. There were a lot of tears, anger and fear while Zach made strides in his recovery. He develops a strong bond with Rafael and learns that he can trust and love again. It was so easy to get wrapped up in Zach’s life and the lives of his rehab companions as they reveal their painful and heartbreaking stories. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I’ve lived eighteen years in a season called sadness where the weather never changed. I guess I believed it was the only season I deserved. I don’t know how but something started to happen. Something around me. Something inside me. Something beautiful. Something really, really, beautiful.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />This beautifully written, powerful story is one of the best I’ve read this year. It made me cry buckets, but I’m happy Zach has a chance for recovery, hope, and a new beginning.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12634731256693666679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I know, it's only been a month and maybe I should add a "so far" but I doubt that there will be a book anytime soon, that will wreck me in such a beautiful way.<br/><br/>You probably heard of Benjamin Alire Saénz.   <span id="freeText6979565273248472573" style="display:none"><i>“The heart can get really cold if all you've known is winter.” </i><br /><br />This is my favourite book of 2017. I know, it's only been a month and maybe I should add a "so far" but I doubt that there will be a book anytime soon, that will wreck me in such a beautiful way.<br /><br />You probably heard of Benjamin Alire Saénz. You loved it. I did, too. But this was even better.<br /><i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i> is deeply moving. It tells Zach's story, Zach who attends therapy every day, who doesn't remember how he even got here, who doesn't know what happened before or why he is where he is. He knows he doesn't want to talk, he's hurting and someone has written <i>sad</i> on his heart when he was born.<br /><br />I picked this book up last night, I put a song on repeat, I read and I read and I finished it at half past one, with wet eyes and and an aching heart and a smile on my face.<br />I love this book.<br /><br />In a nutshell: It's one highly emotional and underrated novel and you should read it immediately because you're missing out on something amazing.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.instagram.com/fantasticbooksandwheretofindem/" rel="nofollow noopener">Find more of my books on Instagram</a></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer693553370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6143748078753587560"><b>Omggg this was so dark and heartbreaking and BEAUTIFUL</b>. I freaking adored it. It's so brutal though and about just how deep the trauma of mental illness, addiction, and pain of being unloved can run. Like excuse me while I turn into that puddle over there.<br/><br/><b>Obviously I don't want to spoil the ending, but IT IS THE BEST ENDING OF MY HEART.</b> I fully nearly gave it 5-stars just for that last chapter alone. I'm c r y i n g. Bless this ending and its perfect measure of darkness and hope.<br/><br/><b>I really loved Z</b></span>
  <span id="freeText6143748078753587560" style="display:none"><b>Omggg this was so dark and heartbreaking and BEAUTIFUL</b>. I freaking adored it. It's so brutal though and about just how deep the trauma of mental illness, addiction, and pain of being unloved can run. Like excuse me while I turn into that puddle over there.<br /><br /><b>Obviously I don't want to spoil the ending, but IT IS THE BEST ENDING OF MY HEART.</b> I fully nearly gave it 5-stars just for that last chapter alone. I'm c r y i n g. Bless this ending and its perfect measure of darkness and hope.<br /><br /><b>I really loved Zach and so so felt for him.</b> So he's in like a rehab facility for alcohol addiction, but he doesn't remember why. I felt the blurb kind of was like "oH HE HAS AMNESIA" but it's more like suppressed memories. He's very vocal about the fact he doesn't <i>want</i> to remember. And like holy heck as he starts being forced to face his past, you know why this kid doesn't want to remember. <b>Seriously this book is heartbreaking.</b> My heart kind of broke several times for Zach...he is so lost and so confused and full of hate and loneliness gahhh.<br /><br /><b>It's also just so downright emotional.</b> SO EMOTIONAL. Like we have a rehab centre where we get to meet a fair few people as well, and all their stories are going to break a rib or two over your heart. But like they cry. At one point, Rafael writes Zach a note and it says something about <i>girls cry</i> but then he crosses it out and says <i>boys cry</i>. BECAUSE IT'S OKAY FOR BOYS TO SHOW EMOTION, AND TEARS, AND HEARTBREAK. I love love love that this was in there.<br /><br />Also Adam, the therapist, was SO FREAKING AMAZING. I love him.<br /><br />(And like, this isn't a critique or anything, but I'm not sure if Zach was closeted [to himself] gay or not? I was unclear about that.)<br /><br /><b>Smol List Of Dislikes:</b><br />• it was a lot of tell tell tell...instead of showing<br />• i think Zach's backstory reveal could've been revealed a little more punchingly?<br />• Zach's thoughts are very repetitious and if I had a dollar for every time he said "that really wigged me out" i would've been freaking rich. grr<br /><br /><b>ALL IN ALL? Seriously one of the most heart-hitting books I've read in a long time.</b> It was beautiful and dealt with mental illness straight and head on. It's not a "nice" book and it's going to lay out the darkness of abuse, addiction, and depression...like really really deep depression. It's messy and the characters make bad decisions. But tHAT ENDING. My heart is so full after that ending!!! I love it!!!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14275154788588080928">I wont give a summary of this book, because that's clearly been done about a hundred times below. What I will do, is tell you what I thought of it.<br/><br/><br/>Anyone who has given this book a high rating clearly has no understanding of addiction or teenagers. The language in this book is tedious and obviously contrived. He uses the same expressions over and over in an effort to sound young, or cool, or edgy, but it mostly just comes off as obnoxious. If I had to guess, the author writes "That really tears m</span>
  <span id="freeText14275154788588080928" style="display:none">I wont give a summary of this book, because that's clearly been done about a hundred times below. What I will do, is tell you what I thought of it.<br /><br /><br />Anyone who has given this book a high rating clearly has no understanding of addiction or teenagers. The language in this book is tedious and obviously contrived. He uses the same expressions over and over in an effort to sound young, or cool, or edgy, but it mostly just comes off as obnoxious. If I had to guess, the author writes "That really tears me up" about 100 times in this book.<br /><br />The plot drags and drags. We don't even find out what the character went through that was so awful until about the last 20 pages of an over 200 page book. To top it off, all the characters that the main character interacts with come off as mildly child-molestery. I think the author is trying to create sympathetic adult characters that care, but the way that he wrote them, I just kept waiting for one of them to stick their hand down his pants. It was unsettling. <br /><br /><br />I don't recommend this book.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Thomas by: </span>
      Nancy
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99543787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7986364641995770212">This was a very dark, depressing, disturbing book. Yet, it was also beautiful and bittersweet.<br/><br/>Zach finds himself in a rehabilitation facility with no memory of his past. He struggles with the monster living in his dreams and inhabiting his thoughts. There are people that help him though - like his therapist Adam, who is always the optimist, and his roommate Rafael, a 53-year-old man with the eyes of a young boy. Through his time at the treatment center he tries to conquer his isolation and vanqu</span>
  <span id="freeText7986364641995770212" style="display:none">This was a very dark, depressing, disturbing book. Yet, it was also beautiful and bittersweet.<br /><br />Zach finds himself in a rehabilitation facility with no memory of his past. He struggles with the monster living in his dreams and inhabiting his thoughts. There are people that help him though - like his therapist Adam, who is always the optimist, and his roommate Rafael, a 53-year-old man with the eyes of a young boy. Through his time at the treatment center he tries to conquer his isolation and vanquish his fear of the monster.<br /><br />Interestingly enough, the author of this book is also a poet. Saenz's writing was very unique - it flowed like effortless poetry rendered into prose, and each page carried great thought and meaning. Zach's story was so, so sad, but there's enough hope in the book to keep readers flipping the pages.<br /><br />Sometimes there was a bit too much metaphor and thought though - a few of Zack's mental wanderings could have been trimmed. There were a few dull moments here and there, but nothing too boring.<br /><br />A very melancholy novel with an ultimately uplifting message about alcoholism and self-esteem.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7986364641995770212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12723022093907258482">4.5 / 5<br/><br/>Trigger warnings up the wazoo with this one, and this is one of those ones that I can't say that I "enjoyed" as much as I can say that I thought it was masterfully crafted. After reading Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe and being rather meh about it, I was unsure how I'd feel about this book. However, someone who went through rehab that I trust recommended it, so I downloaded it and checked it out. <br/><br/>And...this story made me feel a lot. While I haven't ever had a dru</span>
  <span id="freeText12723022093907258482" style="display:none">4.5 / 5<br /><br />Trigger warnings up the wazoo with this one, and this is one of those ones that I can't say that I "enjoyed" as much as I can say that I thought it was masterfully crafted. After reading Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe and being rather meh about it, I was unsure how I'd feel about this book. However, someone who went through rehab that I trust recommended it, so I downloaded it and checked it out. <br /><br />And...this story made me feel a lot. While I haven't ever had a drug or alcohol addiction, I have had addictions of other legal and less harmful varieties and have felt very low and have dealt with some pretty crazy shit. Zachary starts off very apathetic/depressed but is gradually able to move beyond that by eventually recognize his love for others and their love and regard for him. Recognizing, accepting and acknowledging love is one thing I've often had difficulty with in my own life, especially when in the deepest depths of feeling low, so I felt like on some level I could empathize with him. I've also had a difficult year, but for other reasons than Zack. Sometimes, like him, I just needed to cry and cry and cry and nothing really ever felt better but having gone through shitty things before at least I knew that things could and would with time. I might not get the main character in his entirety but I did feel like there were lots of points to latch onto and use as points to relate and empathize with him.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1517795115" />
    <a title="Arch Bala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4261899-arch-bala"><img alt="Arch Bala" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1517795115?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arch Bala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arch Bala" href="/user/show/4261899-arch-bala">Arch Bala</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781933693798" href="/book/show/16566522-last-night-i-sang-to-the-monster">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=omg">omg</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=on-top">on-top</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=drugs">drugs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=depressing-shits">depressing-shits</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4261899-arch-bala?shelf=my-top-2016-reads">my-top-2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1517795115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4033909586276216302">Eighteen-year-old Zach does not remember why he’s in a rehabilitation center for alcoholics, but with therapy and group sessions, he soon finds out that he’s capable of dealing with his past and be able to work on his recovery.<br/><br/>I decided to pick this up when I found out that the author was working on the sequel of his multi-awarded novel Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe. I just wanted to get a feel again, of how this author lured me the way he did with Ari and Dante’s stor</span>
  <span id="freeText4033909586276216302" style="display:none">Eighteen-year-old Zach does not remember why he’s in a rehabilitation center for alcoholics, but with therapy and group sessions, he soon finds out that he’s capable of dealing with his past and be able to work on his recovery.<br /><br />I decided to pick this up when I found out that the author was working on the sequel of his multi-awarded novel Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe. I just wanted to get a feel again, of how this author lured me the way he did with Ari and Dante’s story and this one totally gave me those feelings again! [and this came out three years before Ari and Dante] What an incredible novel! The quality of his writing is impeccable. His characterization rich and the realism behind each character masked through his beautiful prose is fucking genius! Seriously, I cannot even begin to tell you how much I laughed with Zach, cried with him and all those crazy mixed-up emotions that it felt like I was living his life.<br /><br />I believe this story. I devoured everything in it although Zach may have used a few expressions excessively – I believed that these were actually part of his personality where he could not fully articulate himself, depending mostly on his ‘private conversations’ with people.<br /><br />While reading it, I was Zach in the story – not that I’m an alcoholic, an addict or something but there’s something about his character that totally resonates with me. The ‘lost me,’ the ‘little boy’ me that may have been looking for some happiness. I dunno, it’s almost depressing to think about those times while I’m writing this review but yeah, I guess the book is kind of depressing but not in a “I will wallow in this shit and will cry myself to sleep” kind of way. I guess, what I’m trying to say is that you have a choice to read this book and get all weepy about it or read this, wallow in it but expect that at the end of it – there’s some sort of happy ending waiting for you. I liked that in this book. It’s dark in a way that it showed the reality of people suffering from addiction. There was no sugarcoating the fact that it’s not just about destroying themselves but it’s also about destroying everything around them, but most importantly – something I’ve gathered from experience that it’s actually a cry for help.<br /><br />Last Night I Sang to the Monster is an evocative prose of a novel that will leave you enthralled with Benjamin Alire Sáenz artistry! What a truly delightful masterpiece!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4033909586276216302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1310203154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1310203154" />
    <a title="Kaila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35924884-kaila"><img alt="Kaila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533304308p2/35924884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1310203154?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaila" href="/user/show/35924884-kaila">Kaila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=beautiful-writing">beautiful-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=trigger-warning-abuse-or-rape">trigger-warning-abuse-or-rape</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=stay-with-me-forever">stay-with-me-forever</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=mental-illness">mental-illness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=breathtakingly-beautiful">breathtakingly-beautiful</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1310203154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11362204521800535652"><b>4.5/5 stars</b> <br/><br/><b>
  <blockquote>“People who have monsters recognize each other. They know each other without even saying a word.” </blockquote>
</b><br/><br/>Once again, <i>Benjamin Alire Sáenz</i> has rendered me <b>speechless </b>with another beautiful and touching story. His writing is not only full of <b>powerful </b>images and is <b>profoundly poetic</b>, but it also seems to captures feelings and emotions that can't be explained with literal prose. This was a very raw and quite heavy exploration of alchohilsm in a teenager and his experience inside a mental he</span>
  <span id="freeText11362204521800535652" style="display:none"><b>4.5/5 stars</b> <br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>“People who have monsters recognize each other. They know each other without even saying a word.” </blockquote>
</b><br /><br />Once again, <i>Benjamin Alire Sáenz</i> has rendered me <b>speechless </b>with another beautiful and touching story. His writing is not only full of <b>powerful </b>images and is <b>profoundly poetic</b>, but it also seems to captures feelings and emotions that can't be explained with literal prose. This was a very raw and quite heavy exploration of alchohilsm in a teenager and his experience inside a mental health facility. Even though I've never experienced what Zach has, this book was written in such a way that it perfectly captures the feelings of anxiety, lack of identity, loneliness and self discovery that everyone can realte to in some capacity. This was definitely not the most fun book series because of the harsh subject matter, but it also wasn't all darkness as it had very hopeful and heartwarming moments as well.<br /><br />Zach is only eighteen, but he’s not spending his time at high school like the other teens his age. Instead, Zach is at a rehab facility because he’s an alcoholic. He can’t quite remember the chain of events that led him to rehab and he’s not sure if he ever wants to remember, remembering means so much more pain. Zach has the idea that God decides how ever is, and God decided that he would be sad. Sad and lonely. He doesn’t want to talk to his therapist, his roommate is so nice but Zach doesn’t really want to talk to him about anything important either. Everyone has an opinion on how he should live his life, but in reality, he has to figure it out for himself. <br /><br />I really hate my attempt at trying to describe this book, so really take that synopsis with a grain of salt. This book is so much more meaningful and beautiful than I could possibly describe. It’s not about action or major exciting conflicts, but more about an emotional journey for Zach and the other characters we meet along the way. I thought this book was so <b>unbelievably gorgeous!</b> It was heart-wrenching, raw and touching. I used to read (and love) hard-hitting young adult books a lot but I’ve taken quite a long break from them. This book has made me realise how much I actually love these more emotional, mature YA books with quite serious themes.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>“That’s how I feel. I think God is the wind. It’s all like a game to him. Him. God. And it’s all pretty much random. He takes out his pen and starts writing on our blank hearts. When it came to my turn, he wrote “sad.” I don’t like God very much. Apparently, he doesn’t like me very much either.” </blockquote>
</b><br /><br />I also feel like I’d forgotten how much I loved this author’s storytelling. <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i> is one of the most beautifully written, touching and heartwarming books I’ve ever read, a definite favourite. That’s why I have absolutely not idea why I didn’t read more from this author. I’m so happy that I’ve finally changed that because this book was <b>unforgettable</b>. The storytelling was done perfectly as Zach’s past and present fused together perfectly. I think it progressed in such an effortless and natural way which is why I think it was so fantastically executed. Yes, there were times I was frustrated with Zach. But there were also times when I just wanted to hug him and I felt so proud for him. He slowly got better and felt better about himself and it was in no way unbelievable. I don’t really know how to describe what I am trying to articulate, basically <b>I just really love this author and this story.</b><br /><br />I was quite surprised when I first started this book because the writing was so different than the first book I’ve read by this author. I have to admit, it did take some getting used to but I very soon ended up liking it and thinking it was perfect for the book. There were so many times when I read a line that I thought was absolutely perfect. Saenz just seems to articulate things that can’t be said using literal prose. Even though I’m not an alcoholic and haven’t gone through the stuff Zach has, I could still relate and connect to so many lines in the book. I think that’s the <b>real beauty</b> about the writing in this book, it really gets to the core of the coming of age story, regardless of the subject matter.<br /><br />I think this book is sadly underrated. I really think this is one of the best explorations of alcoholism and therapy in YA and yet so many people haven’t heard of it. Beyond that, this is also just one of <b>the most heartwarming and hopeful books</b> that I’ve read in a long time. Don’t get me wrong, there were also a lot of heartbreaking, sad and frustrating moments at all. Basically this book was just one ball of crazy emotions, but I wouldn’t have wanted it any other way. It left me feeling so fulfilled and satisfied, which is all I can ask for in a good book.<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>“All my friends thought I was a very happy human being. Because that's how I acted- like a really happy human being. But all that pretending made me tired. If I acted the way I felt, then I doubt my friends would have really hung out with me. So the pretending wasn't all bad. The pretending made me less lonely. But in another was, it made me more lonely because I felt like a fraud. I've always felt like a fake human being.” </blockquote>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="11362204521800535652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/3654952-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">High School and above</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118768029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14034143315632556006">I have to say that this has quietly become one of my favorite books. The story is about addiction and the walls in one's life that block recovery. It's about trying to forgive in order to live life. I'll admit it was a slow read for awhile, but I think it was a subconscious thing. The danger is that it's too easy to see yourself in the characters that are portrayed and that can be difficult, especially if you've dealt with any kind of recovery in your life. And who hasn't these days? <br/>More than a</span>
  <span id="freeText14034143315632556006" style="display:none">I have to say that this has quietly become one of my favorite books. The story is about addiction and the walls in one's life that block recovery. It's about trying to forgive in order to live life. I'll admit it was a slow read for awhile, but I think it was a subconscious thing. The danger is that it's too easy to see yourself in the characters that are portrayed and that can be difficult, especially if you've dealt with any kind of recovery in your life. And who hasn't these days? <br />More than anything, this book is a love story, but not in the traditional sense. It's about loving oneself and loving others. It's also a story of hope. I've never had a book in which made me cry repeatedly for the last quarter of the book. The author did an incredible job of letting the reader into the minds of the characters. More surprisingly, the book was incredibly poetic, again, not in the traditional sense. I vested so much of myself into hoping Zach would overcome his demons and learn to forgive and live life. This will be a book that will resonate with you for some time. <br />This is not a book for middle schoolers. The language is extreme as is some of the content. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14034143315632556006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText6506395012133327224" style="display:none"><i>“I wondered what it was like to feel whole, to not feel torn up or stunned out or wigged out or any of those things. Those who have an intimate relationship with these monsters, we learn about their confidential suffering and silent pleas for help. In this story, sanity and crazy are what shape the rules of logic.<br /><br /><i>“People who have monsters recognize each other. They know each other without even saying a word.”</i><br /><br />So this book is about Zack, who practically attends a psychiatric facility every day to get better, even though he doesn’t remember how or why he got there, nor what happened before. Even though at first, he doesn’t want to remember any of it… Zack has a very messy mind, full of “useless” papers lying all over it. He’s in deep hurt due the God wrote the word ‘sad’ in his heart. But with time, learns he doesn’t need to feel that way at all;)<br /><br />Also, have I forgotten to mention this was fUCKING BEAUTIFUL!!</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1913286668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11346372066716896353">This book packs an emotional punch that tears right into your heart.<br/>It's an incredibly vivid story that starts off slow and before you know it, has you in its hooks. You start to root for the characters, living along their ups and downs.<br/>It breaks you, and it mends you.<br/>The writing is rough around the edges which only adds to the appeal and atmosphere the further to get into it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10582542763148751590">update: I'm a rare 5 star person. It has to be an overall good book with a decent plot, sympathetic characters, unobtrusive language and a satisfying ending. And above all else, it should be believable. This book had it all for me.<br/><br/>As for a satisfying ending, some people like non-endings, like how "The Giver" ended. Some people, like G.R.R. Martin wants everyone dead and long forgotten as time marches on. I, more or less, like happy endings. <br/><br/>And believable, even if its paranormal/mythical, there </span>
  <span id="freeText10582542763148751590" style="display:none">update: I'm a rare 5 star person. It has to be an overall good book with a decent plot, sympathetic characters, unobtrusive language and a satisfying ending. And above all else, it should be believable. This book had it all for me.<br /><br />As for a satisfying ending, some people like non-endings, like how "The Giver" ended. Some people, like G.R.R. Martin wants everyone dead and long forgotten as time marches on. I, more or less, like happy endings. <br /><br />And believable, even if its paranormal/mythical, there is that suspension of disbelief that you either have it or you don't. <br /><br />I'm not big about picking quotes from a book to sum up my thoughts, but this author, just wow. It's between Zach and Adam, the counselor.<br /><br /><blockquote>"But look, Zach, another part of you lived. You lived, Zach."<br />"You're crying," I said. <br />"That happens sometimes," he said.<br />We both sat there for a long time, not saying anything. <br />"When Rafael was talking about his son, you cried then too."<br />"Yeah, I cried."<br />"Do we hurt you?"<br />"No. You move me, Zach."<br /><i>That's a beautiful thing.</i> That's what I wanted to say, but I didn't say that. I didn't say anything. <br /></blockquote><br />Like I said, I almost didn't get past pg. 15. Tiny print, and just a bunch of sadness. <br /><br />Push through it, and see a boy broken into a million pieces scooped up and slowly reformed. <br /><br /><br />~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br />Almost done with this one, and it almost made me late to work today. <br /><br />Sometimes, a sad book makes you just sad for the world, or sad for yourself. Not this one, and not for me at least. <br /><br />Don't get me wrong: this is a really heartbreaking book. The sadness is just imbued into each and every conversation with the characters and all the internal dialogue.<br /><br />While the thing going on with Zach is that he can't remember what happened before he got there, you just know it's so terrible that his body won't let him remember. Very powerfully written and lyrically beautiful, and this is coming from a book reviewer who doesn't like flowery language and forced prose...but this is just..wow. <br /><br />more to come when I finish today....</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2958431428" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2958431428" />
    <a title="Carol Flores" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18907573-carol-flores"><img alt="Carol Flores" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581399629p2/18907573.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2958431428?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol Flores" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol Flores" href="/user/show/18907573-carol-flores">Carol Flores</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2958431428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16746679055733901179"><b>4.5/5<br/>Trigger warning: domestic violence, drug and alcohol abuse, rape and depression.</b><br/><br/><i>“I have a new theory", he said, "and the theory is this: if I develop a great capacity for feeling pain, then I am also developing a great capacity for feeling happiness.”</i><br/><br/>This quote was one of my favorites and it summarizes the book pretty well. Benjamin Alire Sáenz has been one of my favorite authors since I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1337977905' rel='nofollow noopener'>Aristotle and Dante discover the Secrets of the Universe</a>, and the more I read his novels, the more </span>
  <span id="freeText16746679055733901179" style="display:none"><b>4.5/5<br />Trigger warning: domestic violence, drug and alcohol abuse, rape and depression.</b><br /><br /><i>“I have a new theory", he said, "and the theory is this: if I develop a great capacity for feeling pain, then I am also developing a great capacity for feeling happiness.”</i><br /><br />This quote was one of my favorites and it summarizes the book pretty well. Benjamin Alire Sáenz has been one of my favorite authors since I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1337977905" rel="nofollow noopener">Aristotle and Dante discover the Secrets of the Universe</a>, and the more I read his novels, the more I love and admire him. He has poured his heart and soul into his books and it shows. And if I may add, I don’t know if it’s just me, but his books came into my life in a moment I needed them. <br /><br />This one in particular reminded me that everybody has monsters, and we all struggle with things from our past, whether they’re broken relationships with a lover, with family, friends or even with ourselves. I mean, I’m an adult and there are still things I don’t know about myself, because every day I’m growing for better or worse. But <b>Last night I sang to the monster</b> took me to places I tend to avoid because they touched a sensitive vibe in me. <br /><br />Benjamin writes in a way that it doesn’t matter if you’re 15, 25 or 35, you’re going to get emotional either way. All his characters are too real and especially those who have a big heart, are the ones that had stayed with me ever since. And even when they’re fictional people, I just want to protect them from all the harm in the world. <br /><br />In here we meet Zach, a young adult who suddenly wakes up in a facility where ill people are gathered. I dare to say it’s a psychiatric hospital where people can go and try to get into a program that would help with their struggles. Some of them are alcohol addicts, other are into drugs, or have anger issues. Consequentially, most of them don’t know how to stop hurting themselves one way or another, but they’re there because they want to get better but don’t know how to start or how to deal with the whole process of living with a mental illness, or how to overcome an addiction and stay sober. <br /><br />Except for Zach. He doesn’t even know why he is there. He can’t remember what happen before that. And every time he tries, he shuts down. <br /><br />So, little by little, we get to know his roommates, Rafael and Sharkey; and his doctor, Adam. Rafael is an old man with a smile that can lit up a room, but who has had depression ever since he was a kid; Sharkey, a young man that apparently has it all from his parents, except for love and understanding; and of course, Adam, a doctor that actually cares about Zach and he tries, by all means, to help him understand what happened so he can start healing.<br /><br />Now, I truly don’t want to spoil the whole thing because I think that going blind with this book might be a better choice. Yet, I think this is an accurate representation of how several things affect not only us but also the people that we love. Unfortunately, most of us don’t know how to express ourselves when we’re hurting, or we simply don’t know how to deal or how to react to problems that are not under our control.<br />It’s way too easier to pretend that everything is fine, that we have an amazing life, when in reality we have an enormous hole inside us, and so, to fill it up, we find comfort in drugs, alcohol, sex, or abusive relationships. <br /><br />And I mean, I’ve been there. I truly know what it’s like to feel lost. When I was a teenager I had depression and I tried, by all means, to fill the hole inside me, to feel good enough and worthy. I tried to find love in places that couldn’t even feel it, least give it. I did not love myself back then due to many things, but the fact was that I didn’t understand why I had to be in so much pain. Why couldn’t I be happy? And I mean, how could I? Did I deserve it? Why on earth was I alive if it didn’t matter what I did? I’d still be worthless. <br /><br />Now I know that there was something inside me that was wrong. That I truly didn’t have friends, only people who took advantage of me. And I blamed myself for everything that was wrong, even if it wasn’t my responsibility or my choice. But now that I’m older, I’ve realized that I’m responsible for my own happiness, my own serenity, and what other people do or don’t do, it’s on them. <br /><br />I felt a great amount of pain, and I’ve had to deal with loss and grief. I’ve been lied to, I’ve been manipulated, rejected and insulted by my own family; I’ve had terrible relationships that were never meant to last. But still, after all that struggle, now I know better. Now I’m stronger and I know that everything that happens, both good and bad, is temporary. That it is better to go with the flow and accept the things that I cannot change, but still try to get better on the things that I do have control over. <br /><br />In a few words, this book brought me back to those dark times in my life and it broke my heart once again. I cried, and cried, and cried, to the point that I was like <i>“why can’t I stop? How in the world do I have so many tears inside me?”</i>. And yet, it also gave me hope and a sense of purpose. It reminded me that my story may actually help others. <br /><br />And perhaps that was the purpose of this book anyway. I’m sure Benjamin is the kind of author that has the ability to see us humans in a raw, real way. That’s why he can write so many characters that we can identify with because we’re both good and bad, and compassionate but harmful too. We, as humans, are dual creatures, and we can survive lots of things, but we can also create chaos and destruction among ourselves. The big question is what we decide to do with our lives, and how we cope up with what’s happening outside our heads. <br /><br />I absolutely recommend this book but please, be aware that it deals with dark topics like I mentioned in the beginning of this review. Still, it is one of my favorite books from 2019 and despite all the crying, I cannot wait to read his other novels! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16746679055733901179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_271583570" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271583570" />
    <a title="raya (a little mango)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4535448-raya-a-little-mango"><img alt="raya (a little mango)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546250066p2/4535448.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271583570?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="raya (a little mango)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="raya (a little mango)" href="/user/show/4535448-raya-a-little-mango">raya (a little mango)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4535448-raya-a-little-mango?shelf=mental-health">mental-health</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4535448-raya-a-little-mango?shelf=heavy-boots">heavy-boots</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271583570" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12113184751441972669">I do like and appreciate what Sáenz presents in this novel, and the writing style holds down a nice sense of prose blended with poetic flair and metaphor. At the same time, this story doesn’t grip me—and I mean really, truly grip me—the way other books do. I’m talking about books like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41918.There_Are_No_Children_Here_The_Story_of_Two_Boys_Growing_Up_in_the_Other_America' title='There Are No Children Here The Story of Two Boys Growing Up in the Other America by Alex Kotlowitz' rel='nofollow noopener'>There Are No Children Here: The Story of Two Boys Growing Up in the Other America</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22628.The_Perks_of_Being_a_Wallflower' title='The Perks of Being a Wallflower by Stephen Chbosky' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Perks of Being a Wallflower</a>. Those are books that captivate my attention and emotions and seizes them very firmly. What I’m </span>
  <span id="freeText12113184751441972669" style="display:none">I do like and appreciate what Sáenz presents in this novel, and the writing style holds down a nice sense of prose blended with poetic flair and metaphor. At the same time, this story doesn’t grip me—and I mean really, truly grip me—the way other books do. I’m talking about books like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41918.There_Are_No_Children_Here_The_Story_of_Two_Boys_Growing_Up_in_the_Other_America" title="There Are No Children Here The Story of Two Boys Growing Up in the Other America by Alex Kotlowitz" rel="nofollow noopener">There Are No Children Here: The Story of Two Boys Growing Up in the Other America</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22628.The_Perks_of_Being_a_Wallflower" title="The Perks of Being a Wallflower by Stephen Chbosky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Perks of Being a Wallflower</a>. Those are books that captivate my attention and emotions and seizes them very firmly. What I’m saying is this: Sáenz has what I believe to be a good and powerful story (especially for the YA audience—ages 15/16 and up, though depending on maturity level), but it’s not a story I fully connect to. And to be honest, I don’t think I allowed myself to fully connect. I didn’t want to. <br /><br />Zach’s story is not something I was able to read through in one sitting, or even split into chunks over several days. When I first started reading, I took a week-long break before holding it in my hands again and greeted its text. I think Sáenz does an exemplary job at relaying a proper sadness, anger, anxiety, and even confusion in relation to Zach’s history, but this is what makes it difficult to read. I’ve read stories, both fiction and non-fiction alike, that range in subject matter from sexual abuse, addiction, death, murder… they’re mood dampeners, right? Even watching news can be depressive. <br /><br />I swear, my local news has reported three child deaths and several adult deaths this week with enough sad detail to make me say, “Enough!” I don’t want to watch the news anymore, and I had a similar reaction to <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>.<br /><br />Unlike other stories I have read with similar topics, Sáenz's writing effectively altered my mood sate. In other stories, I’ve read and thought, “Jesus, how horrible,” and then I sympathize. However, <i>this</i> story actually latched on to me and dragged my mood lower, lower, and lower for most of its length. There is a lot of gloomy text to march through, and it wasn’t until Zach took a little more interest in his environment that I became genuinely curious about other characters (e.g., <i>What are their stories? What will happen to them?</i> and so on). Soon after, I found myself turning pages just hoping I could finally piece together what happened to Zach—something he doesn’t remember or want to remember. <br /><br />What bothered me was forcing myself to read through Zach’s refusal to even try to remember. Even though repressing his memory and not wanting to remember are both understandable and realistic, I felt like I was sifting through repetitive “I’m sad/upset/leave me alone/God, I need some bourbon” statements. In a way, this gives a full picture that’s necessary in comprehending Zach’s complexity of character and emotion—to understand why he is the way he is without making undue judgment from an outsider’s perspective. <br /><br />Something happened to Zach, something tragic. I can’t give away what that something is, but when repressed memories and tragic events are involved, it is not likely that an individual will suddenly have a “Great, great! Remembering is fantastic!” moment. It’s a slow, laborious process, and readers journey in baby steps alongside Zach’s progressive course. As a result from this “progressive course,” it is promising to watch Zach learn how to trust and form relationships all the while slowly allowing his barrier to crumble. <br /><br />Stories like Zach’s are not only sad to read about, but to think about, and they often do not end well. Sáenz, with admirable lyrical quality, shows that these stories do not always have to meet lamentable endings. Given, however, the right set of support and assistance—which, I think, is rare to come by compared to a general survey of outcomes. Regardless, it’s a great showcase of how a damaged, broken individual can face the odds and find inner-strength to plow through <i>hourly</i> struggles. Literally. Every day is a struggle—every hour, every minute, can feel like hell. <br /><br />For me, and generally speaking, this story is a great example of facing your “monster” and digging deep to find a will to fight and change. Discovering a new-found sense of will means realizing <i>I <u>do</u> want to live</i> despite the suffering and excruciating hurt that has inflicted life-long damage. While emotional and mental scarring may never completely heal, it’s a matter of perspective and how willing a person is to accept what has occurred and “make the best of it” (so to speak). However, there is a lot more when looking at the specifics to Zach’s story, yet I hesitate to talk about it. (I would hate to spoil anything!) <br /><br />All in all: well-written example of an injured character’s journey toward recovery. Ultimately, this story bestows awareness, understanding, and, I believe, inspiration and hope. If you’re up for handling a dismal climate, I do recommend this book. (Though you may want to consider some lighter material to balance it out—I certainly did.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="12113184751441972669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10519557-cari?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10519557-cari?shelf=read-on-oyster">read-on-oyster</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1325732894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3835844242790825517">Have you ever read anything that put a sick, shook up feeling in your stomach? Like the words you just read put a dark cloud over your head and made you feel not just sad, but slightly ill? That's how I felt for so much of <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>...<br/><br/>This book is <b>heavy</b>, but luckily it is written in this really beautiful, poetic way that allowed me to still enjoy it as a literary work. Also, thank goodness, there is some sun shine peeking through those dark clouds by the end. So much of it</span>
  <span id="freeText3835844242790825517" style="display:none">Have you ever read anything that put a sick, shook up feeling in your stomach? Like the words you just read put a dark cloud over your head and made you feel not just sad, but slightly ill? That's how I felt for so much of <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>...<br /><br />This book is <b>heavy</b>, but luckily it is written in this really beautiful, poetic way that allowed me to still enjoy it as a literary work. Also, thank goodness, there is some sun shine peeking through those dark clouds by the end. So much of it was painful to experience through the eyes of the characters, but that made their triumphs all that much more meaningful. <br /><br />The blurb does a great job of summarizing the book without giving too much away, so I won't venture into the plot in this review. I will, however, drop a few quotes here at the end to show what I mean about how beautifully it was written: <br /><br />"<i>I keep seeing a newspaper being tossed around in the wind. And then a strong gust comes along and the newspaper is thrown against a barbed wire fence and it gets ripped to shreds in an instant. That's how I feel. I think god is the wind. It's all like a game to him. Him. God. And it's all pretty much random. He takes out his pen and starts writing on our blank hearts. When it came to my turn, he wrote sad.</i>"<br /><br />"<i>God, it was incredible to feel so perfect. Look, God didn't write the word perfect on my heart. But cocaine did what God didn't. Wow. Perfect.<br />I was on fire. I mean it. On fire! The truth is that I wanted to die. It would have been beautiful to die feeling so alive. I knew I'd never be that perfect again.</i>"<br /><br />Note to self: If I'm going to spend my birthday reading again next year, pick a less depressing book! ;)</span>
  <a data-text-id="3835844242790825517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1992628399" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1992628399" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1992628399?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/34878679-last-night-i-sang-to-the-monster">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=for-class">for-class</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=mental-health">mental-health</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1992628399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15915223549045265990">3.5 out of 5<br/><br/>While listening to the audiobook version of <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>, a realistic YA novel, at first I couldn't decide whether the book is exceptionally beautifully written or exceptionally beautifully narrated. I think it's the combination of both. Thanks to Benjamin Alire Sáenz's writing and MacLeod Andrews' narration, the main character's, Zach's, voice feels conversational and authentic, and I could understand, relate to, and even feel his pain, anger, sadness, denial, an</span>
  <span id="freeText15915223549045265990" style="display:none">3.5 out of 5<br /><br />While listening to the audiobook version of <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>, a realistic YA novel, at first I couldn't decide whether the book is exceptionally beautifully written or exceptionally beautifully narrated. I think it's the combination of both. Thanks to Benjamin Alire Sáenz's writing and MacLeod Andrews' narration, the main character's, Zach's, voice feels conversational and authentic, and I could understand, relate to, and even feel his pain, anger, sadness, denial, and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ultimately love and happiness <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Furthermore, Benjamin Alire Sáenz's prose is poetic and evocative, and the story is emotionally complex and deeply moving. Although <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i> deals with many depressing issues, it somehow manages to remain hopeful, uplifting, and so BEAUTIFUL, I just can't (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[and I'm not even talking about the ending here, which is sentimental, yes, but also deeply satisfying <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). All the characters are complex, realistic, often messed up in myriad ways, and I loved them all (well, maybe except for Zach's brother, Santiago). Remarkable.<br /><br />The middle of the novel, however, seems a little bit drawn-out, but it might be kind of fitting as the process of recovery and healing takes time, requires a lot of patience and hard work, and rarely is straightforward.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15915223549045265990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2041404553?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Filipa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Filipa" href="/user/show/10622600-filipa">Filipa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2041404553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3378202293599785278">How Zach would put it: <i>That really teared me up.</i><br/><br/>As I started this book, I confess I was expecting nothing less than the simply beautiful Benjamin's writing. I was fast disappointed since what I found was much different. "Last Night I Sang to the Monster" is the story of Zachariah, told with Zach's very own words, changing the tone of the narrative completely from the one we could expect.<br/><br/>However, as much as the first half of the book was hard to get through, beause I wasn't really connecting with</span>
  <span id="freeText3378202293599785278" style="display:none">How Zach would put it: <i>That really teared me up.</i><br /><br />As I started this book, I confess I was expecting nothing less than the simply beautiful Benjamin's writing. I was fast disappointed since what I found was much different. "Last Night I Sang to the Monster" is the story of Zachariah, told with Zach's very own words, changing the tone of the narrative completely from the one we could expect.<br /><br />However, as much as the first half of the book was hard to get through, beause I wasn't really connecting with this book's essence, it still got to me and my feelings. By the end, I was sad, broken, happy and somehow full of hope and possibility.<br /><br />I think this book shows you how all of us are capable of change and improvement toward better days and happiness, and how important it's to let people get in, to heal, to help the change, when we're in a bad place.<br />I think that in such a horrible world and scenario, we saw a lot of love and a lot of beautiful things. And that moved me more than I thought it would.<br />Also, that ending was such a tight hug after so much pain and confusion.<br />Again, my heart is full: of love, of beauty, of hope and possibility.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3378202293599785278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13294314797239284132">Heartachingly beautiful. Full review to follow tomorrow, when I've had time to digest.<br/><br/>This book caught me from the very first line: <i>"Some people have dogs. Not me. I have a therapist. His name is Adam. I'd rather have a dog".</i> I mean, anyone who's ever had to have therapy can sympathise with that. <div id="review_2214712371" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2214712371" />
    <a title="Josiah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1914201-josiah"><img alt="Josiah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239682454p2/1914201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2214712371?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josiah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josiah" href="/user/show/1914201-josiah">Josiah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2214712371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3763562289671923009">Benjamin Alire Sáenz didn't hit the big time until <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i> came out in 2012. He already had four bilingual picture books, three YA novels, and a few collections of poetry and other works for adults, but his career took off like a jet when he started writing Ari and Dante's drama in the Texas desert. Yet people who knew his books before might have had an inkling in 2009 that his career was on the rise, when <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i> hit book</span>
  <span id="freeText3763562289671923009" style="display:none">Benjamin Alire Sáenz didn't hit the big time until <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i> came out in 2012. He already had four bilingual picture books, three YA novels, and a few collections of poetry and other works for adults, but his career took off like a jet when he started writing Ari and Dante's drama in the Texas desert. Yet people who knew his books before might have had an inkling in 2009 that his career was on the rise, when <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i> hit bookstores. The lyrical tone of <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i> is equally present in these pages that tell of eighteen-year-old Zach Gonzalez, a new resident at a rehab facility for troubled individuals. Zach's father was an alcoholic, and his mother clinically depressed. His older brother, Santiago, was unstable and dangerous, routinely threatening their parents and beating Zach so he missed school for days recuperating from the injuries. Zach remembers all that and his own alcohol dependency, but he's blocking out what happened next. How did he wind up in this facility? His therapist, Adam, encourages Zach to open his mind to his recent turbulent past, but Zach resists. He doesn't know why he's rooming with mental patients and addicts, but it must be something horrifying for him to have erased it from his mind. He's safer not knowing what triggered the selective amnesia.<br /><br /> "It's entered into my head that I exist in this in-between space. Maybe that's just the way it is with some people. And there's nothing anybody can do to change it."<br /><br /> —<i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>, P. 11<br /><br /> Rehab could be worse. Zach bunks with Rafael, a fifty-three-year-old alcoholic. Zach used to lean on booze to take the edge off his family problems, and Rafael was driven to drink by his own sad history. The two are far apart in age, but aside from Adam, Rafael is the only person Zach will speak with about personal matters. An artist and poet, Rafael understands battling for sobriety when you can't believe you want anything more than to disappear into forgetting. The two of them room with Sharkey, a twenty-seven-year-old of ample financial means who dominates the conversation when he's around. Zach goes through the motions of getting better in their therapy group while refusing to recall the events that led to his stay here. He'd like to keep this up forever.<br /><br /> "See, I have this theory: some people exist in the world in an old way and some people exist in the world in a young way...some guys, they'll always be like boys in some ways. I don't know if this is a good thing or a bad thing. I haven't decided yet."<br /><br /> —<i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i>, PP. 48-49<br /><br /> The inevitability that Zach's trauma will be revealed is a lit fuse growing shorter with each flip of the page, but it's not the only thing driving the plot. Zach has a monster inside poised to inflict harm once he remembers, but Rafael has a monster too, one he's fought for almost fifty years. The tragedy of his life is heartbreaking because of all he is to Zach, a surrogate father who listens with love and concern. Rafael expresses his own pain through painting and writing, pain that has threatened to drown him most of his life, but he's still alive after all he's endured. Watching Rafael brave the bleak cold of his own personal unending winter scares Zach. Is that what it takes to process and move beyond the hurt, decades of excruciating daily effort? Why should Zach put himself through that when the solace he found in bourbon, numbness leading eventually to death, is so much easier? It's always easier to die than live; the dead require no effort for anything, but neither will they ever enjoy a warm afternoon with the sun on their face, or a walk in the park with someone they love. In his heart Zach doesn't want to embrace nihilism, but its siren song is scary powerful. To break the spell, he'll have to confront what happened that tore apart his family, but remembering will be the hardest part of everything he's been through.<br /><br /> Zach is surrounded by addicts, but they're kindred spirits. Rafael, Sharkey, Amit, Lizzie...they know the sweet thrill of drugs and alcohol, nearly impossible to forgo once you've enjoyed it. Having felt like nothing to his own family, not worthing loving or usually even acknowledging, the alcohol dulled Zach's hurt, the drugs carried him to a place of ecstasy and fulfillment. His first experience with cocaine validated him in a way he couldn't remember ever feeling. "The truth is that I wanted to die. It would have been beautiful to die feeling so alive. I knew I'd never be that perfect again." Whether the impetus is good or bad, after you reach life's high point it's hard to be happy with less. Your heart's desire isn't something you give up without a lot of anguish. When you're different from the world, but the world demands you conform and squeezes you into its mold, how do you accommodate it without damaging yourself? Zach doesn't see how he and the world can be compatible, screwed up as they both are. "What did being connected to the world get you? It got you sadder. <i>Look, the world is not sane</i>. If you stay connected to an insane world, well, you just go crazy. This is not a complicated theory. It's just simple logic." Anyone rational would fear a world that has inflicted grievous wounds on them time and again, as though it enjoyed spilling their blood. Zach is in no hurry to rejoin the scrum of regular life, even if Adam and Rafael insist he'll be capable of doing so once he's dealt with his repressed memories.<br /><br /> If a mentor is what Zach needs, he couldn't have a much better one than Rafael. Rafael helps Zach see when he's lying to himself. There's been a shortage of people who freely love Zach, but Rafael unabashedly fills that need. Zach can't imagine what he'll do when Rafael leaves rehab. How will he handle the monster inside without Rafael's poetic understanding to help keep it at bay? Zach can't avoid it indefinitely, but Rafael has advice for coping. It's like getting caught in a desert storm. "They just come up on you—the wind and the thunder and the lightning and the rain begins to pound. And you think that the world is going to end. It's this overwhelming apocalyptic moment. And then, just like that, it's over. And the world is calm again. And the air smells clean and new. And smelling it, you want to be alive again." In the throes of crisis it feels as though the earth is cracking to pieces under you, and you'll never stand on solid ground again. The storm seems strong enough to blast you and your world apart, but rarely is it as devastating as it feels in the moment. The horror will pass, probably sooner than you believe in your panicked state. A clean new world will emerge for you to venture into, and you'll find there's still room in it for your happiness.<br /><br /> "I wish I didn't have a heart that God wrote <i>Sad</i> on", Zach laments to himself. Some of us do seem to have that word engraved inside us. We deal with it each in our own way; Zach notices that Rafael puts his sadness into his painting. Creating art obviously hurts Rafael, though; pain fills his face and tears run down his cheeks as he paints. Why does he grapple with the monster through art if it's so distressing? Rafael's explanation is a turning point for Zach. "I've been hurting most of my life. I tried to pretend I wasn't. I even believed my own lie. I've lived my entire life trying to avoid pain, Zach. That's a terrible way to live. I don't care any more if it hurts." Will it ever stop hurting, Zach asks? "I don't think so...If I'm working on a painting, and it doesn't hurt, then the painting won't matter. And if it doesn't matter, then it isn't real—then <i>I'm not</i> real." Why does it have to hurt, Zach presses? "I have a new theory...if I develop a great capacity for feeling pain, then I am also developing a great capacity for feeling happiness." Life hurts us all, but excising the potential for pain prevents us from feeling joy, too. What is life worth if your ceiling is too low for joy, even if your floor keeps you from falling into fear and misery? You need to participate in life to the max, regardless of which emotion God has written largest on your heart that will always threaten to be your undoing. With support from the people you love, someday you can inscribe words of your own choosing on your mended heart.<br /><br /> It's not a popular opinion, but I think <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i> is better than <i>Aristotle and Dante Discover the Secrets of the Universe</i>. Both have beautiful word flow, thoughts that dance in my head with rhythm and verve. The relationships in this book culminate in a payoff near the end that stirs the emotions as every great novelist is capable of doing. That was the moment I decided to round my two-and-a-half star rating up instead of down, and I considered giving the full three stars. We all hit roadblocks to happiness and success, some harder to surmount than others, and <i>Last Night I Sang to the Monster</i> compassionately and honestly walks us through these crises. Benjamin Alire Sáenz's narrative voice is as soothing as Rafael's, and he points us in the direction of hope with no pretense that slaying our own monsters will be anything but the biggest challenge of our lives. That, my friend, is worth a read for just about everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3763562289671923009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
